this trick alone is insufficient to address the conditional jumps and other schemes of AuraStealer's indirect control flow obfuscation. Nonetheless, given how many calls are obfuscated with this simple scheme, applying it still provides meaningful progress and can serve as a solid starting point for further deobfuscation. Moreover, AuraStealer applies the same obfuscation scheme to hide certain function arguments as well, making it applicable for those cases as well.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo tackle the more sophisticated patterns, \u003cstrong\u003ebackward slicing\u003c/strong\u003e proved to be the most effective approach. For those interested in a more detailed explanation of how this technique works, the articles \u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lummac2-obfuscation-through-indirect-control-flow\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eLummaC2: Obfuscation Through Indirect Control Flow\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e by Nino Isakovic and Chuong Dong, and \u003ca href=\"https://cyber.wtf/2025/11/19/rhadamanthys-loader-deobfuscation/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eRhadamanthys Loader Deobfuscation\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/a\u003e by Melissa Eckardt provide excellent explanations and are definitely worth reading.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn short, backward slicing is a program analysis technique that identifies instructions that may influence a specific register or memory address at a specific program point by following data and control dependencies. The resulting set of instructions (also referred to as “slice”) can then be used for further analysis, such as emulation or symbolic execution, to recover the value of the targeted register or memory location.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our case, we employed a heuristic heavily inspired by backward slicing but simplified and tailored specifically for AuraStealer. The heuristic can be summarized in the following steps:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eLocate the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e instruction. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAdd the register used as the target operand of this indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e instruction to the \u003ccode\u003etracked_regs\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eset.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIterate through instructions in reverse (from the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp/call\u003c/code\u003e towards lower addresses), analyzing each instruction whether it affects any of the tracked registers.\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eIf an instruction \u003cstrong\u003emodifies\u003c/strong\u003e a tracked register, mark it as \u003cstrong\u003erelevant\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFor a relevant instruction, determine if it completely overwrites a tracked register (e.g., \u003ccode\u003eMOV reg, imm\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003eXOR reg, reg\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e,\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eetc.), and if so, remove that register from \u003ccode\u003etracked_reg\u003c/code\u003e, ending its dependency chain.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eOtherwise, identify all source registers of that relevant instruction and add them to the \u003ccode\u003etracked_reg\u003c/code\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/em\u003eset to propagate the dependency further back.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eStop the process if any of these conditions occur:\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe \u003ccode\u003etracked_regs\u003c/code\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eset is empty (all dependencies have been resolved).\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA control-flow instruction is encountered (\u003ccode\u003ecall\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e, \u003ccode\u003eret\u003c/code\u003e), indicating a basic block boundary.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA maximum instruction limit is reached (fail-safe).\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFinally, return the address of the earliest (lowest address) relevant instruction found, marking the start of the slice.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor clarity, this heuristic is not meant to implement complete backward slicing. It deliberately omits memory‑write tracking and focuses solely on register flows. However, it is important to note that this limitation is intentional, as register-level analysis is sufficient for the deobfuscation of the indirect control flow obfuscation employed by AuraStealer. We use the heuristic solely to identify the slice’s starting address, from which we then perform symbolic execution with Angr to compute the target address(es). That said, if AuraStealer’s obfuscation were to change, the heuristic could be readily extended to also account for memory interactions.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe reason we chose symbolic execution over emulation is that symbolic execution is inherently designed to explore all possible execution paths, making it easier to implement. In terms of computational requirements, it remains feasible since we only symbolically execute small code chunks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttentive readers may have noticed that this approach has a minor imperfection. The heuristic terminates upon encountering a \u003ccode\u003ecall\u003c/code\u003e instruction. However, as shown in Figure 5, the target address of some \u003ccode\u003ejmps\u003c/code\u003e can depend on the results of previous function calls, which may lead to an incorrectly identified slice start. To handle such cases, whenever a register is unconstrained, we assign it a symbolic boolean value (0 or 1), under the assumption that any function influencing such jumps returns a boolean value. Such cases, however, are relatively rare.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe whole process can be automated with a bit of scripting and the recovered target addresses can be pushed back into IDA as comments, which already gives a good baseline for navigating the code.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=xVeq5AHc 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":867},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=8dhhCtuk 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":580}],"caption":"  Figure 11: Recovered target addresses set as comments in IDA Pro.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_11_0.jpeg.webp?itok=8dhhCtuk","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eHowever, we can go further – our real goal is to make IDA reconstruct the original control flow graph, not just annotate it. Once the target addresses of the indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmps/calls\u003c/code\u003e are retrieved, there are several ways to proceed. One option would be to patch the binary. However, due to the variability of the patterns involved and the presence of an anti‑tamper protection in AuraStealer, we have opted for a different approach.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eInstead of patching the binary, which is destructive and easily detectable by malware, we leveraged the IDA SDK to hook directly into the instruction analysis process. The idea is simple – rather than letting IDA analyze the instructions on its own, we intervene in the analysis process and modify how the instructions are decoded. For this purpose, IDA provides \u003ca href=\"https://cpp.docs.hex-rays.com/structpost__event__visitor__t.html\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003epost_event_visitor_t\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, a class that allows listening and reacting to specific events in IDA by overriding the virtual function \u003ccode\u003ehandle_post_event\u003c/code\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=86TkldZY 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":107},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=TnbqbesL 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":72}],"caption":"Figure 12: The declaration of the handle_post_event virtual function.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_12.jpeg.webp?itok=TnbqbesL","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eAmong all the possible event codes, the interesting one is the \u003ccode\u003eprocessor_t::ev_ana_insn\u003c/code\u003e\u0026nbsp;(analyze instruction), which is triggered right after IDA calls the processor module’s instruction decoder, but before the resulting \u003ccode\u003einsn_t\u003c/code\u003e structure is finalized and committed to the database. At that point, we can inspect, or even modify, the decoded instruction. In other words, we can alter IDA’s perception of the code without ever touching the underlying binary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt this point, several observations are worth highlighting:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe indirect calls almost always have a single target.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eMany conditional jumps have only two targets, one of which is the immediately following instruction.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eResolving the indirect call obfuscation is therefore straightforward. Whenever we intercept the \u003ccode\u003eprocessor_t::ev_ana_insn\u003c/code\u003e event, we check whether the analyzed instruction is an indirect call (\u003ccode\u003ecall reg\u003c/code\u003e). If the target address is known and it is the only possible target, we replace the \u003ccode\u003ecall reg\u003c/code\u003e instruction with \u003ccode\u003ecall \u0026lt;calculated_target_address\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor indirect conditional jumps, it would, in principle, be possible to determine the exact branch type by analyzing the conditional move used to compute the target address and replacing the \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with the corresponding conditional jump instruction. However, we opted for a much simpler and more pragmatic strategy. Many of these indirect jumps are, in fact, only obfuscated loops or \u003ccode\u003eif-then/if-then-else\u003c/code\u003e constructs with one of the target address pointing to the immediately following instruction. So, whenever we encounter an indirect \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with precisely two possible targets, one of which is the fall-through address, we replace the \u003ccode\u003ejmp\u003c/code\u003e instruction with \u003ccode\u003ejnz\u003c/code\u003e (jump if not zero) pointing to the non-fall-through target address.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis transformation does not yield a perfectly accurate decompilation. However, if we accept the fact that some comparisons may appear as\u0026nbsp;\u003ccode\u003econdition != 0\u003c/code\u003e rather than their exact form, we end up with a control flow that is already much easier to follow. Moreover, whenever the precise condition semantics matter, the original condition can always be examined directly in the disassembly. Although it would be possible to reconstruct and substitute the exact conditional jump for every case, we do not believe it is worth the effort.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_13.jpeg.webp?itok=DA2cm_PF It is thus often necessary to manually adjust where the function begins and ends to ensure IDA analyses the whole function.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eException-Driven API Hashing\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo obfuscate its usage of WinAPI functions, AuraStealer employs the API hashing technique. For these purposes, it first resolves all the functions required by the binary via PEB-walking and builds two hash-based lookup tables that maps hashes to XOR-masked addresses.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe reason for maintaining two tables is that AuraStealer does not invoke these function directly. Instead, it deliberately triggers an exception, which, however, is intercepted by a custom exception handler. The handler inspects the address from which the exception originates and uses that information to dispatch the appropriate function address.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo resolve a specific function, AuraStealer takes the precomputed \u003ccode\u003eMurmurHash3\u003c/code\u003e value (seeded with \u003ccode\u003e0xDEADBEEF\u003c/code\u003e) of the function’s name, re-hashes it with \u003ccode\u003eFNV-1a\u003c/code\u003e, and uses both values to query the first precomputed lookup table. This query returns the XOR-masked address of the actual target function, again along with the mask. Depending on that outcome, it either performs or skips additional checks, which include an anti-tampering check and a dialog box check.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-Tampering Check\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe anti-tampering check is done using the \u003ccode\u003eMapFileAndCheckSumW\u003c/code\u003e WinAPI function, which computes the file’s checksum and compares it against the value stored in the PE header. If those values differ, AuraStealer stops its execution.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe purpose of this check is to prevent analysts from patching or otherwise altering the executable. With that in mind, analysts should be cautious about any changes made to the binary (including software breakpoints) and remain vigilant during dynamic analysis, as the malware may deliberately exhibit misleading behavior if tampering is detected.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDialog Box Check\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSimilar to what \u003ca href=\"https://outpost24.com/blog/lummac2-anti-sandbox-technique-trigonometry-human-detection/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eLumma\u003c/a\u003e, and more recently also \u003ca href=\"https://research.checkpoint.com/2025/rhadamanthys-0-9-x-walk-through-the-updates/\" target=\"_blank\"\u003eRhadamanthys\u003c/a\u003e, does, if AuraStealer detects that it is being run unprotected, it displays a dialog box prompting the user to enter a code to continue. Unlike Lumma and Rhadamanthys, it requires the user not only to click the OK button but also to correctly enter the displayed code. On the other hand, it does not display any warning message that malware is to be run, which is what both Lumma and Rhadamanthys display in the message box.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=S_XSIdFH 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":262,"height":149},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=LE2ksud8 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":262,"height":149}],"caption":"Figure 27: The AuraStealer’s dialog box displayed when running unprotected.","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2025-12/aurastealer_figure_27.jpeg.webp?itok=LE2ksud8","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThis mechanism halts AuraStealer’s execution until the correct value is provided and is aimed at forcing the malware distributors to deliver the stealer with an additional protective layer. The script is publicly accessible on our GitHub.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eHuman Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe human check comprises two subchecks, both implemented as infinite loops.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first check monitors whether the user input changes over time. It begins by measuring the number of ticks between two \u003ccode\u003eGetTickCount\u003c/code\u003e calls with a \u003ccode\u003eSleep(1000 ms)\u003c/code\u003e in between. A while loop then repeatedly performs a \u003ccode\u003eSleep(500 ms)\u003c/code\u003e followed by a call to \u003ccode\u003eGetLastInputInfo\u003c/code\u003e, which returns a \u003ccode\u003eLASTINPUTINFO\u003c/code\u003e structure. This structure contains a variable, \u003ccode\u003edwTime\u003c/code\u003e, representing the tick count at which the last input event occurred. Then, another call to \u003ccode\u003eGetTickCount\u003c/code\u003e is made, and \u003ccode\u003edwTime\u003c/code\u003e is subtracted from it. If the result is less than the previously measured number of ticks measured by the one-second \u003ccode\u003eSleep\u003c/code\u003e, the check passes. Otherwise, the loop repeats until the check passes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe second check begins by a call to \u003ccode\u003eGetForegroundWindow\u003c/code\u003e, which returns a handle to the current foreground window. A while loop then repeatedly calls \u003ccode\u003eSleep(500 ms)\u003c/code\u003e and checks \u003ccode\u003eGetForegroundWindow\u003c/code\u003e again. If the returned handle differs from the initial one, the check passes. Otherwise, the loop continues until the foreground window changes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAnti-VM Checks\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Anti‑VM check consists of multiple subchecks. First, the presence of a hypervisor is checked using the \u003ccode\u003ecpuid\u003c/code\u003e instruction. When \u003ccode\u003ecpuid\u003c/code\u003e is executed with \u003ccode\u003eEAX = 1\u003c/code\u003e, the 31st bit of \u003ccode\u003eECX\u003c/code\u003e indicates the environment – it is \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e on a physical machine and \u003ccode\u003e1\u003c/code\u003e on a virtual machine.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNext, a combination of the WinAPI functions \u003ccode\u003eGetDesktopWindow\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eGetWindowRect\u003c/code\u003e is used to obtain the \u003ccode\u003eright\u003c/code\u003e variable from the resulting \u003ccode\u003eRECT\u003c/code\u003e structure, which is then compared against \u003ccode\u003e1024\u003c/code\u003e (it is expected to be greater). Generated Mutex\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo prevent multiple instances from running simultaneously, AuraStealer utilizes dynamically generated mutexes based on build-specific and time-dependent values. The malware first loads the \u003ccode\u003ebuild_id\u003c/code\u003e value from its configuration (for example, \u003ccode\u003ea0858933-16a7-433f-a9cc-68490ace0576\u003c/code\u003e) and computes a \u003ccode\u003edjb2\u003c/code\u003e hash over it. The hash is then added to the result of an integer division \u003ccode\u003e_Xtime_get_ticks()/36000000000\u003c/code\u003e, which effectively produces a value that changes once per hour. The final sum serves as a seed for a Mersenne-Twister pseudorandom number generator, which is used to generate a random alphanumeric string between 16 and 32 characters in length. has discovered a novel WhatsApp account takeover campaign that we refer to as GhostPairing Attack. On the surface it looks very simple. Victims receive a message from one of their contacts, usually something along the lines of:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e“Hey, I just found your photo!”\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=IU7aPJ2I 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=4sVSkl_U 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":376,"height":264},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=3Fdaa8Rj 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=4DqpaZk8 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":376,"height":264},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=c9aJ7I0A 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=lPLNu5fy 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":376,"height":264}],"caption":"Lure message received by the victim","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/1.png.webp?itok=c9aJ7I0A","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe message includes a link that appears as a Facebook style preview. When users open it, they see a page that imitates a Facebook viewer and asks them to “verify” before they can see the content.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat follows is a small sequence of steps that many people complete without thinking. At the end of that sequence, an attacker has full access to the victim’s WhatsApp account without stealing a single password.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis article explains what we observed, how the attack works in practice, which infrastructure is involved, and what users can do to protect themselves.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe use the term GhostPairing Attack because the victim is tricked into completing WhatsApp’s own device pairing flow, adding the attacker’s browser as an invisible extra device on their account. There is no password theft or SIM swap – instead, the user approves the attacker themselves by entering a pairing code that looks like normal verification.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat we observed in the wild\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign was first noticed in Czechia, where compromised accounts started sending short, informal messages to local contacts. The wording varied slightly, but the structure remained the same. The messages were brief, usually mentioning a photo, and included a link that rendered as a Facebook element inside WhatsApp.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=oGbEG2yY 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=85v0XbWP 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":386,"height":242},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=k3pAs_YA 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=1tOUP9yY 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":386,"height":242},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=AbtY1QCB 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=B_abS2P5 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":386,"height":242}],"caption":"Another lure message sent via WhatsApp","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/2.1.png.webp?itok=AbtY1QCB","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eThe domains behind these links were not Facebook at all. Instead, they belonged to a cluster of lookalike sites with names related to photos or posts, for example:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003ephotobox[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003epostsphoto[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eyourphoto[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ephotopost[.]live\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eyourphoto[.]world\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003etop-foto[.]life\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003efotoface[.]top\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003ePaths often included strings like /login/post.com or /login/facepost.com, which helped maintain the illusion that the user was dealing with some kind of Facebook login or content viewer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlthough our earliest sightings are in\u003ca\u003e Czech\u003c/a\u003e, nothing in the attack depends on the language. The same template can be reused for any country simply by changing the text of the lure.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThe fake viewer that is not about Facebook at all\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen a user clicks the link within the WhatsApp message, they land on a very minimal page. It usually carries the Facebook logo and colors, a stripped down layout and a button that invites them to continue or verify before they can see the photo.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis page has two purposes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;First, it creates a sense of familiarity that encourages the user to trust the page. People expect Facebook to ask for some kind of confirmation from time to time. Seeing a login button or a verification step feels normal.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSecond, it acts as the attacker’s control panel. The page is not connecting with Facebook but rather mediating between the victim and the legitimate WhatsApp Web infrastructure that the attacker is abusing.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDepending on the variant, the next screen either:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eshows a QR code and tells the user to scan it with WhatsApp, or\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eshows a numeric code and tells the user to enter it into WhatsApp to confirm a login.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eBoth flows lead to the same outcome, but later we’ll show why the QR code is rarely used in favor of the numeric code. The attacker gets their browser registered as a linked device on the victim’s WhatsApp account, where they have complete control over personal messages, photos and everything else a person does on the WhatsApp platform.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eA quick primer on WhatsApp device linking/pairing\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo understand the abuse, it helps to look briefly at how device linking in WhatsApp is \u003cem\u003esupposed\u003c/em\u003e to work.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen people use WhatsApp Web or the desktop client, they are not creating a separate account. They are linking a new device to their existing account. WhatsApp offers more than one way to do this:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe one most people know: open WhatsApp, scan a QR code shown on the desktop or browser, and approve the new session.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA less visible option: link a device using the account’s phone number and a numeric pairing code that the user has to confirm in the app.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eBoth are legitimate features, and both are meant to make it easier to connect a new laptop or browser without constantly re-entering credentials. Additionally, both can be repurposed if someone convinces the account owner to cooperate.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign we analyzed does exactly that.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eQR based \u003c/strong\u003e\u003ca\u003e\u003cstrong\u003elinking\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e – possible, but a fringe scenario\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn paper, attackers could run the same scam using WhatsApp’s QR based device linking. In our tests, this works technically, but it is much less practical for real victims.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo abuse this flow, an attacker would open WhatsApp Web in their own browser, capture the QR code shown there and embed it into the fake Facebook viewer page. The victim would then be told to open WhatsApp, go to Linked devices and scan that QR in order to “view the photo”. From WhatsApp’s perspective, the account owner has just approved a new linked device, and the attacker’s browser receives a fully authorized session.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn practice, however, there is a big usability problem. Most people run both WhatsApp and the browser on the same phone. Scanning a QR that is displayed on the very device you are using is awkward at best, and often impossible without a second screen, another phone or a tablet. We have all seen edge cases where this happens in legitimate contexts, but it is not something you would build a mass scam around.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is why, in the activity we have observed, attackers rely on the phone number and pairing code flow instead. It produces the same end result but feels like a normal two step verification process and can be completed entirely on a single device, which makes it far more attractive for large scale abuse.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAbusing phone number and pairing codes\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe numeric code variant is slightly more complex and arguably more dangerous, because it resembles two factor authentication even more closely. It is also the most common way the scam plays out.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eHere, the fake page encourages the user to enter their phone number. It then acts as a proxy to the real WhatsApp device linking endpoint.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe behind-the-scenes sequence looks like this:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe victim types their phone number on the fake page.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe page forwards that number to WhatsApp’s legitimate “link device via phone number” feature.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eWhatsApp generates a pairing code that is meant to be seen only by the account owner.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe attacker’s site takes that code and displays it back to the victim with text that suggests they should “enter this in WhatsApp to confirm the login and see the photo.”\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe victim opens WhatsApp, sees the pairing prompt and enters the code, believing they are completing a security check.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom the victim’s point of view, this feels like a standard verification process. They have seen similar flows in many other services.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom WhatsApp’s point of view, the owner of the account has just approved a new linked device using the correct code.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAgain, the end result is that the attacker’s browser becomes a trusted device for that account.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/3.png.webp?itok=-qmnBLwA account","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/5.jpg.webp?itok=oxvz3Q2r","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat the attacker can do once linked\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter their device is linked, the attacker does not need to exploit anything else. They have the same capabilities that any user has when connecting WhatsApp Web on their own computer.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn practice, this includes:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eReading historical conversations, subject to what has been synced.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eReceiving new messages in real time.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eViewing and downloading media such as photos, videos and voice notes.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCollecting sensitive information shared between friends: addresses, email addresses, codes, links, which can later be misused or resold.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003esending messages to individuals and groups as if they were the victim.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eforwarding the same lure to many contacts with very little effort.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not traditional account hijacking in the sense of changing passwords or locking the owner out. The phone continues to work normally. Many victims are unaware that a second device has been added in the background, which is what makes the scam even more dangerous – criminals are hiding in your account, watching your every conversation without you even knowing it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our observations, that restriction does not consistently clear the linked session. Unless the victim goes into Settings and removes unknown devices, the attacker may retain access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHow victims then become GhostPairing attackers\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe propagation mechanism is simple and effective because it is rooted in real relationships.Once an attacker has access to one account, they can use it to distribute the lure text that we mentioned previously to that person’s contacts and groups. Family chats, school groups, sports teams and work-related conversations are all opportunities.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRecipients see a very short, informal message coming from someone they know. There is no unfamiliar phone number, no obvious formatting errors, no long explanation. This minimal style typically avoids suspicion.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome recipients click, some do not. Those who do and complete the linking step become new compromised accounts. They, in turn, can be used to reach fresh sets of contacts that the original attacker did not know existed – the attack expands like a snowball.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is what makes the campaign efficient. It does not rely on cold spam. It rides existing trust.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhy this approach is concerning\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSeveral aspects of this technique are worth highlighting.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst, it does not rely on stealing secrets. There is no password phish, no SMS interception, no direct authentication bypass. Everything happens inside the boundaries of the feature set that WhatsApp intended.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSecond, the lure is highly plausible. For many users, the idea that “Facebook wants you to confirm something in WhatsApp” does not sound obviously wrong. Codes and QR scans have become part of everyday online life, especially on mobile.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThird, it creates persistent access. Linked devices do not disappear when the user closes a tab. They remain active until manually revoked. If there is no habit of checking the list of connected WhatsApp devices, an attacker can remain connected for a long time.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFinally, it opens the door to more than just simple spam. With access to full conversations and media, an attacker can learn how people talk, who is important to them, and what they might respond to. Voice notes and photos can later be reused in fraud attempts and impersonation, including deepfake enhanced scams, or extortion.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn other words, this is a clear example of how social engineering and legitimate infrastructure can combine into a very effective compromise method.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eInfrastructure and kit-like behavior\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eLooking at the domains and templates involved, there are strong signals that the GhostPairing Attack is driven by a reusable kit that attackers can buy and distribute rather than a one-off project.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe same layout appears across multiple domains. The same idea of a Facebook viewer repeats, with minimal changes. Domain names follow a similar pattern around photos and posts, with a mix of generic TLDs.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom an operational point of view, this is convenient. If one domain is blocked or reported, it can be discarded and replaced with another one using the same files. A buyer of such a kit can plug in their own configuration – for example text targeting a specific region or, as the scam propagates, text specific to an individual –\u0026nbsp;and start sending lures.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat people can do to protect themselves\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor individuals, the most important actions are simple and do not require technical knowledge.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst, it is worth checking which devices are currently linked:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eOpen WhatsApp.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eGo to \u003cstrong\u003eSettings → Linked Devices\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eReview the list of active sessions and log out of anything you do not recognise.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eDoing this once will remove any sessions already created by this sort of scam. Doing it periodically helps catch future problems earlier.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=3oqdxM2K 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=ZdaZZhqk 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":308,"height":632},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=qfOdWwwC 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=Eh2UXPXh 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":308,"height":632},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=b9OMAJ5w 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=rwOhVwhN 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":308,"height":632}],"caption":"Linked devices showing an unknown browser session","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/6.jpg.webp?itok=b9OMAJ5w","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eSecond, treat any request to scan a WhatsApp QR code or enter a numeric code from a webpage as suspicious. WhatsApp linking should happen from inside the app and in response to a deliberate action, not because some external site tells you that it is required to view a file.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThird, enabling Two Step Verification in WhatsApp adds an additional barrier to other kinds of account abuse. It is not a complete solution, but it reduces the range of what attackers can do once they are in.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFinally, sharing information about this kind of scam with family members and group chats is surprisingly effective. Many victims click because they have never heard of anything like this. Once people know that “I found your photo” messages can hide an account linking trick, they are less likely to follow the script.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWhat platforms could improve\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile user awareness is essential, there are also changes that platforms can consider.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eClearer messaging around device linking would help. If users saw large, unavoidable text explaining that they are about to give a new device full access to all messages and media, some would hesitate.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBetter context on linking and pairing events would also be useful. Showing device type, browser, approximate location, and the fact that the request originated from a website, not from the app, could nudge users to cancel when something feels off.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRate limiting device linking attempts, especially those that involve many different phone numbers from the same infrastructure, would raise the cost for attackers. Auto revoking recent linked sessions when spam or abuse is confirmed on an account would shorten the window of exploitation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of these measures alone would eliminate such attacks, but together they would make them less attractive.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBeyond WhatsApp: pairing as a broader attack surface\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eGhostPairing is not just a quirky one off trick in a single app. It is a case study in what happens when a pairing flow is abused at scale. WhatsApp is a natural target, with billions of users and a growing role in everyday and business communication, but the pattern behind GhostPairing is more general.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eConceptually, the same risk exists anywhere you have:\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003eA QR code, code entry screen, or approval prompt that lets a new device connect to an existing account.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA trusted primary device that scans or approves that request.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eA resulting session that is long lived and largely invisible to the user.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf those three conditions line up, an attacker who can control the QR code or pairing prompt, and convince the victim to interact with it, has a realistic path to creating their own ghost device.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMany modern services follow this pattern in one form or another, from messaging apps with web or desktop clients, to collaboration tools, social platforms and account ecosystems that rely on “approve on your phone” prompts. Most of them add extra checks, such as device details, locations, or number matching, which makes abuse harder but does not change the basic dependency: a single distracted click or code entry on the primary device can grant an unknown session elsewhere.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe are not claiming that GhostPairing style campaigns are already running across all these ecosystems. What we can say is:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe design pattern that made GhostPairing possible is not unique to WhatsApp.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAny platform that combines very easy pairing with low visibility of linked devices gives attackers something to work with.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn that sense, GhostPairing should be read as a warning, not just a WhatsApp incident. The more our digital lives depend on quick QR scans and “approve on your phone” flows, the more important it becomes to design these steps so that a single moment of inattention does not quietly create a ghost device that lives in the background for months.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConclusion\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign described here illustrates a subtle shift in how some attackers operate. Instead of breaking cryptography or circumventing authentication, they use the product as designed and persuade users to cooperate at just the right moment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBy abusing WhatsApp’s device linking features and wrapping them in a familiar looking flow, they achieve a clean and persistent account compromise with minimal technical work.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe technique is not limited to one country or one platform. Any service that allows device pairing through QR codes or numeric codes is a potential candidate for similar abuse.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eGhostPairing Attacks are a good example of how social engineering and legitimate features combine into very effective compromises. The attacker never breaks encryption, they simply convince the user to invite them in as a linked device.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe good news is that the defense is well within reach. \u003ca\u003eA combination of user awareness, simple hygiene checks and modest platform changes can significantly reduce the impact of this type of scam.\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"17","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2025-12/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_2842949%20%281%29.jpg?h=fa1c963e\u0026itok=YUxoElQf","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2025-12/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_2842949%20%281%29.jpg.webp?h=fa1c963e\u0026itok=9jsuoSM8 fake shops fake trust\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eModern fake shops are not just about flashy discounts. They are designed to look legitimate at a glance.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMany use polished templates and SSL certificates, so the little padlock icon appears in the browser. That padlock only means the connection is encrypted, not that the site is honest, but scammers know people trust it.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome fake shops seed third party review platforms with positive reviews to boost their scores. In our analysis we saw cases where those reviews were later removed by the platform for breaking guidelines, a strong sign they were fake. In at least one case, the scam site ran on a subdomain of an older, existing site and showed Black Friday deals alongside those disputed reviews. Piggybacking on an established domain is another way to look more credible.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn social media, scammers run ads that look like any other promotion in your feed. They often target specific demographics, interests or locations, and in some cases even reply to comments or reviews to keep up the illusion of a real business.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=QLUIVc2J 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=agIb8F8t 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":1170,"height":1310},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=-_5gWcKZ 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=hes3V86N 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":890,"height":997},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=-ziiI-sN 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=376uSf7c 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":690,"height":773}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-12/removed_reviews.png.webp?itok=-ziiI-sN","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch2\u003eHow to Spot a Fake Online Shop\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThere is no single trick that catches every scam, but a few simple habits can dramatically lower the risk:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003col\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCheck the address bar, not just the logo. \u003c/strong\u003eLook closely at the URL. Watch out for strange spellings, extra words, or domains that do not match the brand name you expect.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBe skeptical of “too good to be true” discounts\u003c/strong\u003e. Genuine retailers do run big Black Friday sales, but if a high demand product is half the usual price, treat it as suspicious.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eVerify reviews\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eDo not rely only on the testimonials shown on the shop itself. Search the store name together with words like \"reviews\", \"scam\" or \"complaints\". Be wary if you only find a handful of overly positive comments.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCheck contact and return information. \u003c/strong\u003eLegitimate shops make it easy to see who they are and how to reach them, for example a physical address, company name, clear return policy, and real customer service options. Vague contact details or only a web form are warning signs.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUse security tools and safer payment methods. \u003c/strong\u003eEnable browser protection and make sure your security solutions are on so known scam domains are blocked before you land on them. When you pay, prefer options that offer buyer protection, such as credit card or trusted payment service, and avoid bank transfers, gift cards or cryptocurrency for online purchases.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ol\u003e\u003cp\u003eScammers treat Black Friday, Cyber Monday and the entire holiday shopping season as a business opportunity. They use the same tools, platforms and marketing channels as legitimate stores, just with a very different goal.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe good news is that you do not need to stop shopping online to stay safe. A bit of skepticism, a habit of checking before you click, and the right security tools in the background will go a long way to keep your money and data out of their hands.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"7","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2025-12/GettyImages-1316468770.jpg?h=790be497\u0026itok=FA7772G8","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2025-12/GettyImages-1316468770.jpg.webp?h=790be497\u0026itok=EVNDAt0d of convenience: How APTs are beginning to work together ","created":"1763590902","subtitle":"Gen Threat Labs uncover evidence of rare cross-country coordination between Russia’s Gamaredon and North Korea’s Lazarus","authors":[{"socialLinks":[],"bio":"A group of elite researchers who like to stay under the radar.","displayName":"Threat Research Team","title":"Threat Research hacking groups typically operate in isolation, each advancing its own nation’s goals. That’s why any sign of collaboration between them is cause for concern. Yet new evidence uncovered by Gen researchers suggests that two of the world’s most aggressive advanced persistent threat (APT) actors, Russia-aligned Gamaredon and North Korea’s Lazarus, may be operating on shared infrastructure.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis discovery hints at something much bigger than mere technical overlap. It points to a possible new stage in cyber conflict, where geopolitical alliances are mirrored in shared digital operations.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFrom allies on the battlefield to partners online\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eRussia and North Korea have maintained a long-standing partnership rooted in shared political and military interests. Moscow backed Pyongyang during and after the Korean War, and in 2024 both nations renewed that alliance through a Comprehensive Strategic Partnership that includes mutual defense commitments.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSince 2022, Pyongyang has stepped up its support for Moscow, formally recognizing Russian-claimed territories in Ukraine and reportedly supplying munitions and troops. In 2024, Reuters reported that North Korean soldiers had been deployed to fight alongside Russian forces in Ukraine, a striking example of the two countries’ deepening cooperation.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow, we may be witnessing a digital extension of that alliance. On July 28, 2025, Gen’s internal monitoring systems detected a suspicious event linking \u003cstrong\u003eGamaredon\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003eLazarus\u003c/strong\u003e activity through a shared IP address. The implications are significant: two state-backed actors from different countries may be coordinating at an operational level.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis development aligns with broader patterns highlighted in the \u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/insights/reports/threat-report-q3-2025\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eQ3/2025 Threat Report\u003c/a\u003e, where state sponsored operations showed increasing sophistication, coordination, and diversification of infrastructure. While those observations were confined within national ecosystems, the Gamaredon–Lazarus overlap suggests that similar dynamics may now be emerging across national boundaries.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eBackground\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ch3\u003eGamaredon\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGamaredon \u003c/strong\u003eis a Russian-aligned APT active since at least 2013, primarily focused on cyber espionage. In 2021, the Security Service of Ukraine issued a \u003ca href=\"https://ssu.gov.ua/en/novyny/sbu-vstanovyla-khakeriv-fsb-yaki-zdiisnyly-ponad-5-tys-kiberatak-na-derzhavni-orhany-ukrainy\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003epress release\u003c/a\u003e, attributing several members of the group as part of Russia's Federal Security Service (FSB) 18th Information Security Center. Since its official inception, the group is believed to have conducted more than 5000 cyber-attacks, most of which targeted Ukrainian government agencies. However, with the onset of war in Ukraine, ESET \u003ca href=\"https://www.welivesecurity.com/en/eset-research/cyberespionage-gamaredon-way-analysis-toolset-used-spy-ukraine-2022-2023/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003ereported\u003c/a\u003e that Gamaredon expanded its operations to include NATO member states, likely aiming to disrupt military aid to Ukraine, underscoring the group’s prioritization of hybrid warfare.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eLazarus\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLazarus \u003c/strong\u003eis a state-sponsored threat actor active since 2009 and widely believed to operate under North Korea’s government. Initially focused on cyber espionage and destructive attacks, Lazarus later shifted toward financially motivated operations to fund future campaigns. In 2021, the United States Department of Justice \u003ca href=\"https://www.justice.gov/archives/opa/pr/north-korean-regime-backed-programmer-charged-conspiracy-conduct-multiple-cyber-attacks-and\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eindicted\u003c/a\u003e three members believed to be part of the Lazarus group, connecting them to North Korea’s Reconnaissance General Bureau (RGB), the country’s primary intelligence agency. With the rise of cryptocurrency, Lazarus increasingly targeted digital assets, as evidenced by high-profile breaches such as \u003ca href=\"https://www.fbi.gov/news/press-releases/fbi-identifies-lazarus-group-cyber-actors-as-responsible-for-theft-of-41-million-from-stakecom\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eStake.com\u003c/a\u003e ($41 million), \u003ca href=\"https://www.fbi.gov/news/press-releases/fbi-identifies-cryptocurrency-funds-stolen-by-dprk\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eAtomicWallet\u003c/a\u003e ($100 million), \u003ca href=\"https://www.businesstoday.in/technology/news/story/wazirx-hacked-north-korean-hackers-behind-235-million-theft-from-indian-investors-report-reveals-439240-2024-07-29\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eWazirX\u003c/a\u003e ($235 million), and \u003ca href=\"https://neobanque.ch/blog/bybit-1-4-billion-hack-lazarus-group-2025/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eBybit\u003c/a\u003e ($1.4 billion).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhere \u003cstrong\u003eGamaredon spies, Lazarus steals\u003c/strong\u003e, but both ultimately serve their governments’ strategic interests.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe discovery: a shared digital footprint\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eJust one day after the \u003ca href=\"https://www.reuters.com/business/aerospace-defense/russia-starts-first-moscow-pyongyang-passenger-flights-decades-2025-07-27/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eannouncement\u003c/a\u003e of new direct flights between Moscow and Pyongyang, Gen identified indicators of a potential collaboration between the Gamaredon and Lazarus APTs. On July 24, 2025, our system tracking Gamaredon’s Command-and-Control (C2) servers via known Telegram and Telegraph channels blocked an IP address:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e144[.]172[.]112[.]106\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=9QhO-lvd 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=9cTN8qMb 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":647,"height":27},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=Xhx1_gKk 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=PS7yGsb1 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":647,"height":27},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=TFWMla6Q 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=rs0aLUYf 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":647,"height":27}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-11/1.png.webp?itok=TFWMla6Q","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eFour days later, during a routine check, the same server was found hosting an obfuscated version of \u003cstrong\u003eInvisibleFerret \u003c/strong\u003e(SHA256: `128da948f7c3a6c052e782acfee503383bf05d953f3db5c603e4d386e2cf4b4d`), a malware strain attributed to \u003cstrong\u003eLazarus\u003c/strong\u003e. The payload matched Lazarus’ tooling and was delivered through an identical server structure (URL: `http[://]144[.]172[.]112[.]106/payload/99/81`) previously seen in \u003cem\u003eContagiousInterview\u003c/em\u003e, a Lazarus campaign that targeted job seekers with fake recruitment messages. While the IP could represent a proxy or VPN endpoint, the temporal proximity of both groups’ activity and the shared hosting pattern indicate probable infrastructure reuse, with moderate confidence of operational collaboration. Whether Lazarus leveraged a Gamaredon-controlled server or both actors shared the same client instance remains unclear, but the overlap is too close to ignore.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eImplications for the global threat landscape\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eCross-country collaborations in the APT ecosystem remain exceptionally rare. The last widely acknowledged example dates back to 2014 with the \u003ca href=\"https://theintercept.com/2014/11/24/secret-regin-malware-belgacom-nsa-gchq/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eRegin\u003c/a\u003e malware, reportedly co-developed by the U.S. National Security Agency (NSA) and the U.K.’s Government Communications Headquarters (GCHQ).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf confirmed, the Gamaredon–Lazarus overlap would represent the first known case of \u003cstrong\u003eRussian–North Korean cyber collaboration\u003c/strong\u003e in the wild.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSuch a partnership could have wide-ranging implications:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eOperational synergy:\u003c/strong\u003e Lazarus’s expertise in monetizing cyberattacks through cryptocurrency theft could help Gamaredon fund or conceal future operations.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStrategic alignment:\u003c/strong\u003e Russia, facing mounting economic and military pressure, could benefit from North Korea’s established infrastructure for covert financial operations.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEscalation potential:\u003c/strong\u003e This kind of collaboration blurs the line between espionage, sabotage, and organized cybercrime, expanding both nations’ offensive reach.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNot an isolated case: national ecosystems are merging\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile cross-border APT collaboration is rare, cooperation within national ecosystems has become increasingly common.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eLazarus x Kimsuky\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eKimsuky \u003c/strong\u003eis another North Korean APT group. It has been active since around 2012 and assessed by \u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/mapping-dprk-groups-to-government\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eMandiant\u003c/a\u003e to operate under the RGB. The group specializes in advanced cyber-espionage campaigns, primarily targeting government entities and consumer-facing organizations.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring analysis of Lazarus’ \u003cem\u003eContagiousInterview\u003c/em\u003e payloads, Gen researchers found that an IP address (216[.]219[.]87[.]41) later reappeared in Kimsuky-linked payloads (e.g., cce27340fd6f32d96c65b7b1034c65d5026d7d0b96c80bcf31e40ab4b8834bcd). This suggests infrastructure reuse or coordination between RGB units, evidence of alignment at North Korea’s national level.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDoNot x SideWinder\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.amnesty.org/en/latest/news/2021/10/togo-activist-targeted-with-spyware-by-notorious-hacker-group/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDoNot\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003ca href=\"https://cdn-cybersecurity.att.com/docs/global-perspective-of-the-sidewinder-apt.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSideWinder\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eare state-sponsored APT groups believed to have been active since 2013 and 2012, respectively, both with ties to the Indian government and a primary focus on cyber espionage.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eGen identified a DoNot-attributed payload (8bb089d763d5d4b4f96ae59eb9d8f919e6a49611c183f636bfd5c01696447938) that later executed a known SideWinder loader (f4d10604980f8f556440460adc71883f04e24231d0a9a3a323a86651405bedfb). The victim was located in Pakistan, consistent with the typical targeting profile of both groups. Historically, cross-country APT collaborations have been exceedingly rare, with only a handful of confirmed cases such as Stuxnet and Regin. This potential partnership signals a shift toward more complex and unpredictable alliances, where geopolitical interests may drive operational convergence.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile the Lazarus–Gamaredon case stands out for its strategic implications, the observed intranational collaborations, such as Lazarus with Kimsuky and DoNot with SideWinder, are equally important. These partnerships demonstrate a growing trend of resource sharing and tactical alignment within national ecosystems, amplifying the reach and resilience of state-sponsored campaigns.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor defenders, these findings underscore an urgent need to adapt detection strategies beyond single-actor attribution. Shared infrastructure, overlapping TTPs, and modular malware frameworks mean that traditional attribution models may fail to capture the full scope of risk. Security teams must:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEnhance infrastructure correlation analysis to detect cross-group overlaps early. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePrioritize intelligence sharing across organizations and sectors to identify emerging alliances. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eImplement layered defenses capable of mitigating diverse tactics from multiple threat actors leveraging common resources.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe era of isolated APT operations is fading. As adversaries evolve through collaboration, defenders must respond with equal agility and cooperation to safeguard critical
